Since I use my bike to get everywhere though, I do find I go out and do a lot more, given that the effort required is lower

E-bikes should be a specific solution to personal "problems" bikes possibly can't solve. For example, I live in a super hilly area.. Commuting uphill (on a road) or around and about everyday on a normal bike is not fun and simply too exerting (not physical condition related). I do want to commute more with my bike - and commuting uphill on an e-bike is like riding the bike on a flat surface. You still get cardio, on a comfortable pace. An e-bike will NOT stop you from having a good exercise on it! I love doing forest roads, offroad etc, but don't do MTB as a sport (downhill trails or jumps, tracks, etc). It has solved all of my location based issues. Just damn brilliant. And I believe that's how everyone should look at e-bikes. Does it solve a problem for you or make you happy, enable you to do more of what you wanted to? If yes, get it. If no, you don't need it! Cheers

Nice to see your progressing reaction over the course of the video. I can only tell you: I also ride mtb and emtb depending on the time I have and the people I'm riding with. Nevertheless, I use my ebike more often as it actually opens up riding opportunities whenever there would not be enough time or I'm afraid of feeling too exhsusted (before or after the ride). I also have two little kids and sometimes it is really hard to squeeze in a ride as the days are long and crammed. If it wasn't for my emtb I would be exercising much less. You don't come home and have to recover for the rest of the day but spend time with your family. And it is still great training, even cardio, as it mainly takes away the peak heart rate sections in my experience. Most of the time I pedal just as hard as on a regular bike. And it made me a better rider: It's much easier to session sections. Moreover I love the exploration aspect: With my emtb I just spontaneously ride up stuff and look at things that I'd usually not consider taking a look at. I do a lot of local riding right at my doorstep and I have come to know my way around very well. I also like not taking my car to go to various riding spots and try to make it a bike tour whenever it's possible. So yeah, apart from the weight I really love my emtb. E-bikes eat legs.  I was a middle-distance (200-1200 km) road cyclist, but when I reached 75 I decided I needed a little help and put a hub motor on one of my bikes.  Well, it was great: I'd done my last 200k at 70, but now I could manage a 240k with 1500 metres of climbing.  A couple of months on and I could still manage 112k on one of my 'honest' bikes: however, doing decent distances again with the motorized beast (aka the eBrute) was more fun.  Three years on and I now have a motor on the 'honest' bike.  Slinging >20 kilos around is wearing, though, so two weeks ago I bought an entry-level road bike with no motor, and I've done a couple of rides on it.  Back when I was doing distance rides I could cover 300km per day: yesterday I did 50 km and today I'm wasted.  I seem to have damaged a quad, too.  Moral of the story: don't buy an e-bike unless there's no other way of achieving what you need to do. And if you do, ride an unmotorized bike as often as you can.

This is more to the commenters: Stop with the e-bikes are only for those that are older or physically limited. Elite athletes are using ebikes to allow for consistent zone 2 workouts in steep or technical terrain. They work so well this way, they allow for a long and consistent zone 2 workout that is the critical aspect for endurance training, all without having to be on a trainer or boring terrain. My cardio has improved using ebike as an intentional training tool although my anaerobic and burst power has def decreased. If you can not afford one I strongly recommend the boredom of a trainer or dedicated zone 2 time if you want to improve cardio. They also make it much more fun to ride with partners of differing fitness and uphill skill level. They make is so much easier for those still learning as they are not exhausted by the up and thus unable to physically and mentally focus on the down - so many beginner and intermediate crashes are bonk related. They are amazing for rehabbing injuries where torgue needs to be limited especially knees. They are incredible if your only access is a steep fire road that would otherwise be a miserable anerobic grind or hike a bike, while that is fun it will limit your desire to do it back to back days. And yes they are incredible for aging or otherwise limited bodies that still have all the desire and bike handling skills and want big days but just can't. I have both, I use both, I love both. That said I am a much bigger fan of lightweight SL ebikes that handle more like analog on the down but do require a significant pedaling the entire time. Not super interested in a 55lb 750w unless I am going to the Alps. The reality is that ebikes will soon be the majority of new medium and high end MTB bikes sold and will likely be 80% of that huge sector within 5 years so even if you hate them you should prepare to wrap your brain around that. The industry already has.

No doubt, for certain individuals, an ebike is a Godsend, and good for them. If the batteries are as large as scooters though, there is a very good reason to prohibit them in many places as they pose a serious fire hazard. The likelihood might be low, but battery fires are especially difficult to extinguish and generate highly toxic gases. Battery fires require expensive specialized firefighting equipment which many places don't have. Unfortunately, batteries can easily be damaged, dramatically increasing the risk of fire.

The invention of so called e-bikes is all about human lazyness. We don't like to struggle to hard. I say so called e-bikes, because we don't name it as what it in fact is; it's bikes with motors and that is well, motorcycles or mopeds. Make a thought experiment; assume we replace the electric motor with one that runs on gasoline but with same power output and max speed and all other technical details alike, so it's comparable in every way with e-bikes. What would we call a bike like that, a "gasoline bike"? No, it would of course be called a motorcycle or moped. We fool ourselves, just name something electric  and all critical thinking disappears. That being said, I think it's good if people with health issues, young or old, ride with motor assistance. But I think it's silly when I meet people in good shape and health who ride e-bikes just because it goes faster and is not so hard. And it's a shame with all the kids on e-bikes, they should build a strong health instead with normal bikes.

Mountain Biking, like surfing, is a "wholistic" sport that pits man against nature the entire activity.  Can I climb that hill without stopping?  Can I make that gap?  Adding a motor is a different sport for weaker people, and when you have to share the same trails, like a surfer must share the same waves, it's annoying as hell to see hordes of weaklings clogging the trails, disobeying conventions.   E-bikes are pushed for one reason, and it's the revenue stream.  They're the latest hot ticket for the beer-gutted, insecure weekend warrior who's aching to be seen riding the same rig as their neighbor.  And don't get me started on all corrupted Strava KOMs.  Please note this is not directed at riders who have a legit medical need for an e-bike.  At 53, I'm sure I'll need one at some point, hopefully later than sooner.
For those w/ a legitimate need, Ebikes are a real game changer and I fully support them.   Not saying those who don't need them shouldn't have them. TL;DR: now that I have an e-bike, I ride just as hard, and I also ride more frequently .   I appreciate your thoughtful commentary. Just a quick note about my own experience: I've got very good data on my mountain bike rides going back nearly a decade - time on bike, heart rate, caloric burn, etc. - and what my data tells me is that my heart rate numbers and my caloric burn haven't changed now that I ride an e-bike. They are pretty much the same. Notably, however, my time on bike has gone way up! In other words, I'm still riding just as hard, but I'm getting more riding in and therefore I'm getting more exercise.. by about 20%!! The reason, for me, is plain. In the past, if I was feeling tired or less than energetic, I would often find myself just not going out. Now, the barrier is so low that there's simply no reason not to go biking if I'm tired. And invariably once I'm on the bike I ride just as hard as I always do.   When I first got the e-bike (Trek Fuel exe) I kept my regular bike (Santa Cruz 5010) for a while, thinking I would go back and forth between my normal Pike and my e-bike. After the Santa Cruz sat for about 2 months without me touching it, I just sold it. Haven't missed it once. Granted, my local trails are e-bike friendly, so I don't have that issue.  Of course, I'm not saying this experience is going to be universal. But it definitely has been mine.

There is a compromise... instead of getting a mid drive based mountain bike get one with a rear hub drive that you actually have to use the gears to pedal with the motor proving only some of the assistance at low speeds in lower assist levels. my ebike has a 3 x 7 gearset with a range from 24 : 28 in first gear to 42: 13 in 21st gear with a 32 tooth midrange. that being said I can put it in a low level of assist and actually use the pedals to get around only having the motor kick in if my speed drops below 7 mph in PAS 1, such as when you hit a particularly tough incline. I know the gear range isn't as wide as true mountain bikes but it's great for what is basically a fat tire bike. Hey, whatever makes it fun for you, then you have exactly what you need.  When I got on the north side of 55 years old, the ol' furnace didn't burn as hot, and I was burned out on decades of working out.  My mountain bike hang in my garage for almost 2 years unused (I had skiing and dirt bikes, but nothing to fill the leg strengthening I needed year round).  Bought an e-mtn bike, and that changed.  Uphills became fun.    But, what was really weird was that when I increase the assist, I actually find myself pedaling harder, simply because I just want to go faster (came from decades of riding/racing MX so when the speed increases, the fun increases).  Very strange.  Got to the point (been riding it for 3 months....have over 700 miles of trails riding) where I can run lowest boost or shut boost off for the entire ride.  So, it gave me my fitness back.  But, when I feel tired on a climb and I increase that boost, I end up peddling like a mad man, getting even more of a workout.   I have a fitness watch, and my heart rate will actually increase on boost assisted climbs.  Never expected that.  Seems to be the opposite of this guy.  We're all different.

I‚Äôve always been an avid cyclist, primarily focusing on road biking. This year, I turned 60, and I‚Äôve noticed it‚Äôs becoming more challenging to tackle long rides due to strong winds and steep hills. So, I decided to get an e-bike. I used to think e-bikes were a bit of a cheat, so I never considered buying one before. However, I found a company that makes fitness e-bikes that look just like traditional bikes. I ended up purchasing a Velotric T1ST from a local bike shop. Here are my thoughts on it.  Velotric has changed the frame sizes for the T1ST and T1ST Plus models, which was disappointing since I own both. The T1ST comes in Small and Medium, while the T1ST Plus offers Regular and Large sizes. However, the Plus's Regular feels more like a Medium/Small, and the Large seems like a Large/Medium. One major upgrade on the Plus is the rear wheel, which has 36 spokes compared to the 28 spokes on the original T1ST. As a 250-pound rider, I broke four spokes in the first month of using the original, so I‚Äôm having the rear wheel rebuilt with stainless steel alpine spokes for added durability. In terms of performance, I got about 36 miles on the original in Echo 5 mode with an average speed of 16 mph. On the Plus, I have to use Trail 3 mode to match the Echo 5 performance, and I‚Äôm seeing around 40 miles with an average speed of 16.6 mph. I also purchased the Velotric Range Extender battery, but it doesn't extend the range as expected. Instead, I often have to turn off the bike to allow the Range Extender to recharge the main battery while I pedal. I typically activate it at the start of my ride and once the main battery runs low, I turn off the bike to let the Range Extender do its thing, then switch it back on periodically until either the ride is done or the Range Extender is depleted. Additionally, the Plus model features 170mm crankarms, while the original has 165mm. With my 29-inch inseam, I need 165mm or 160mm crankarms, so I had to buy third-party options since Velotric doesn‚Äôt sell them. Overall, I prefer the Plus model, but the original was simpler to use.
I thought getting an ebike was going to make me less fit, but the exact opposite happened, mind you, I use it for transportation most of the time, but what happened is that it replaced tram and car rides alltogether. Additionally, the ebike just allowed me to up the comfot a lot on the bike, whereas before I'd have to use skinnier tires and try to keep my bike much leaner, and a more aggresive position, adding 5kg worth of comfort goodies, i.e. an air front fork, bigger tires, a chunky built in lock, dynamo hub, better mudguards, a frame bag with all the tools, parallelogram suspension seatpost, alarm, a nice kickstand, a big removable basket that clicks on the back rack, that can hold my backpack and/or groceries, etc... it's a much heavier bike but it is sooooo much more comfortable. I can go the same speed on my road bike without a backpack, but he ebike is what made it a true car/tram replacement possible.  The other day I drove it to another city to visit my sister just for fun, took 3:30 hours, whereas the folding bike+train wouldve been 50 minutes, and the car 45min, but it was a very enjoyable ride with nice scenery

I have a commuter oriented ebike(with mtb styling) that I use on trails occasionally plus I have a "manual" trail MTB that I'm building. As a bigger guy, the ebike has helped me develop better cardio conditioning because it's a torque sensor style just like the high end emtb's. With torque sensor ebikes, you still input a ton of leg power because you have to move a heavier bike(65lbs for my bike) and I find that I'd put in the same effort with my legs per stroke, but because the bike adds power, I get up the climbs faster and therefore am doing probably 25-30% less work over the whole climb. But that 25% translates to me enjoying my ride longer overall, and still conditioning me for my manual mtb. They are definitely equalizers is some ways, but that also opens the sport to people who normally wouldn't have gotten into mountain biking. More people = more trails, better trails(yes maybe busier trails at times), but ultimately the sport stays relevant for more people too.

I have over well 15,000 miles on my $100 Walmart hyper mountain bike converted to e-bike with a bafang mid drive conversion kit i built back around 2011-12,  i fully upgraded everything on on the bike as everything Walmart failed except the frame within the first year, had to weld and alter rear swing arm for, cassett wheels and disk brakes, used but good rock shocks gas front end, trigger shifters, the works!  The computer resets every winter going months without my battery plugged in so it actually has more miles then that but i had to go by last i checked a few times when it reset, but i average minimum of 1500 miles a year as it is my main transportation. I backpack my 20ah 52v lifepo4 connected via a flixible monster car audio wire and xt90 connector as to not get my battery stolen when i chaine up the bike, also i killed 2 batteries due to ruff terrain, the spot welded connections break and prismatic cells get flattened out and damaged do to shocks from bumps. The trails in this video are far smoother than the roads in Youngstown ohio, lol. Backpacking the battery also helps  make the bike feel way lighter!!! The original bafang ESC failed a few years in which was a common issue back then and they had a new upgrade, other then that the electrics are bullet proof, i did crimp connect and seal the motor and battery leads, dont use the supplied connectors for the heavy wires, Shortly after breaking 4-5k on it i had a gear failure and had to tear down the motor/drive assembly to replace a gear do to cheap grease and not much if it, so it was running  dry, i drilled a hole that i can pump a shot of marine grease in on the gear with my needle tip grease gun every year since, it doesn't need much and you dont want it in the windings. When i got the bafang kit it was $500 and totally worth every penny, after all in with upgrades had less then $1500 in it ... Probably double that with bidenflation! But i do go through 1-2 kevlar back tires (worn to the belts), like 2-4 tubes, a chain and sprocket sets every year too, and a front tire every 2-3 years... Unfortunately I do think the motor isn't going to be lasting long because it's starting to make some weird noises on and off, I honestly just need to start fresh because it's getting in bad shape, all the weathering and aluminum frame is squeaking, ive been babying it cuz i cannot afford to replace it any time soon! Now at 71 years old, managing osteoarthritis in both knees could have meant giving up cycling altogether if not for my upgraded Trek Marlin 5 gen2. Installing the CYC Photon middrive electric motor has transformed my Marlin into a vehicle of renewed freedom. Your comment that hit home most for me was the "its like I'm doing a different sport". I'm really happy to see so many commenters pointing out how their ebikes have got them in the saddle or kept them in the saddle - that has been true for friends of mine locally. Despite these well voiced sentiments I have lamented the direction the sport has been headed for some time - my mountain bike social media is essentially become exclusively race oriented or free-ride oriented content with more and more of the perception of what mountain biking is or means being connected to lift serve style riding or focused on statistics like "the number of laps I rode in a day". When I watch the industry pushing ebikes through advertising and influencer content towards able bodied folks just looking to go faster out in the woods, or go equally fast with less effort, it feels like the sport is flirting dangerously close to the electric dirtbike market that is rapidly evolving to meet it half way (I say this as someone that owns a Suzuki 250). Some motorcycle suppliers have already started offering mountain bikes (it helps that much of mountain bike tech was borrowed from the moto-world often by brands that cut their teeth in moto racing like Renthal and Fox) and I think its telling that Fort Nine is working to expand their media content into mountain biking. I think conversations like the honest one you just posted need to be out in the open to ensure that how this sport changes isn't being driven by cash hungry execs trying to make money on the next trend - but is really a reflection of people having good old fashion fun in the woods with friends.... and hopefully still sweating a bit to earn their turns. A big part of the future of our sport is going to be about the optics of what this sport is and what trails it deserves to have access to.

eBikes are the reason I now ride traditional mountain bikes. Some friends put me on a regular MTB and took me on the trails for the first time, and I was out of shape going up hill, constantly getting stopped on rocks and roots since I didn't know how to pick uphill lines, and ended up pushing the bike as much as riding it, and decided I hated mountain biking. After some time I decided to try again with an ebike, and bought one used. Suddenly I could keep up with my friends and could get over rocks and roots that would have otherwise stopped me in my tracks and made me walk the rest of the way up hill. After doing that for awhile, I got in shape, and learned how to ride and pick lines and anticipate gear changes. Now I ride my (manual) full suspension bike all the time on the trails, and love mountain biking. I still take the eBike out once and awhile when I feel like bombing around some trails (especially helpful when I want to ride after a long day at work, and wouldn't ride otherwise because of time/fatigue). eBikes are very good for those learning, because they can give you an advantage both in a fitness level and technical skill level, and can be very forgiving and get you up most things, and teach you how to ride and love the sport!
I think that e-bikes make most sense for MTB'ers who ride in the tracks that this video features, be it a bike park or just non-artificial trails. It allows the riders to do many more descents (3x at least) than with a regular MTB. Of course when going down they don't behave exactly as a similar MTB, but it's perfectly valid as a training.  On the other side, unless you are NOT slightly fit, e-bikes do not make sense at all for commuting or riding in flat terrain. They are even worse than a regular bike because they are way heavier, and you need the motor to get them moving after stopping.
Your Enduro and your Cross-Country bikes are exactly the same except a small difference in tire profile. What's the point? For someone who doesn't have 20k to throw away?
I kind of relate the fitness side of it to my job. I'm a landscaper, so i work 6 months a year doing incredibly strenuous stuff and getting into great shape, but in the off-season i end up putting on a lot of weight and being very lazy. I can only get so unfit, however, before the landscape season kicks in again and i'm whipped into shape. It's easy to work out 10 hours a day when it's your job and you enjoy it, but it's hard to run joylessly on a treadmill in a gym. Analog mountain biking brings a level of enjoyment that offsets the labour of it. You don't notice how hard you're working. For me, that's the key to fitness. If i got an ebike, i'd suddenly be able to get all of the enjoyment of biking with none of the work. I think it would ruin the symbiotic relationship between mountain biking and my cardio fitness.
E bikes can bring more people to the hobby and more people to the trail. Not sure I want that though.

As someone who usually rides natural trails, often in the form of big days out in the Alps, I started out being open minded about ebike(rs) en gradually became more annoyed by them. From my perspective it's often very discouraging to be working hard while someone else is having a breeze. Then when it gets tough, their bikes are usually too heavy to walk/carry through sections of bad tech. Which makes it dangerous for them. Another truth you really hit home, is that in 90% of cases, people will be enjoying the motorised help, will not be putting out intensities that maintain/increase fitness and they'll plateau their fitness or gradually get weaker. Then there's the added cost of maintenance and the constant worry about whether or not the battery will last and where/when to recharge the batteries. In anything but the most controlled environments close to a trail head, ebikes often show themselves to be a worry and liability.  Sure they have advantages in the way of getting more people out there. But it is a different sport altogether and experience has made me less and less keen on going on rides with ebikers while on a regular self powered bike myself. Online it's often pretty bad as well. I now have a mid-motor  bike and I do not tear up the trails and I am able to climb over 3000 feet elevation and explore more than 20 miles distance.  This isn't possible on battery alone. Maybe they hate I am old and able to reach the places that only strong young guys can get to.  I never use the throttle and only ride on assist 1.  literally 250-300 watts with a 65 lBS bike on the hard stuff but still some hate me.  Not everyone treats me this way but I have experienced it.  Young guys skidding on the down-hill runs are doing far more damage to trails than I could even do if I was full -throttle on the up-hill side. E-bikes need regulation: Watt limits and speed limits. Everyone thinks limits and regulation will ruin the fun, but it actually goes the opposite way. Limits will help ease concerns about e-bikes and help acceptance -- by accepting limits on your e-bikes capbilities, you'll be removing limits on where you can use it. E-bikes shouldn't be letting people go 3x faster. Let's be real here, if you want to go faster than 10mph up a trail, you should do it under your own power. For those of us who started mountain biking in the olden days when pros basically used the same technology for xc and downhill and also uphill races (uphill climb used to be an event). I'm talking John Tomac and Ned Overend days. As an amateur rider and racer, cleaning a technical long uphill trail was more rewarding than ripping a fast downhill section. You needed the skill and the legs. Not that I had exceptional amounts of either but that was what I strived for. You had to pedal up to go down. Fitness, endurance, skill, etc. If you petered out you walked. I rode a single speed  rigid Karate Monkey and lived to ride past people on full suspension race bikes during races. Fast forward to downhill bike parks with lift sevice that are specifically designed to cater to enduro and freeride bikes. Without a dropper post you can't  ride  the bikes correctly. Stretched out, super steep seat angles for when you do climb but awkward on flatter pedaly technical trails. Great bikes going down, can be pedaled uphill when necessary and fun to ride. But these are expensive and have a lot of parts requiring attention and maintenance. Bikes that cost 3 grand fall into the budget category. Onto the ebike which seems at least marginally related to the enduro bike. More speed, more comfort, the ability to blast through technical trails and so on. The appeal of an ebike for someone with an injury, disability, health condition etc. is understandable but for me using the the assistance of a motor so I don't get as tired, suffer less or don't have to stop on a climb to suck wind doesn't get me jazzed. The challenge to withstand physical  discomfort, push the cardiovascular limits and hurt a bit is part of the appeal. If Diana Nyad made the swim from Cuba to Florida  with floaties and a little motor on her back I think something would be lost. Brett Wolfe is a cyclist that raced 24 hr events and he has one leg. He pedaled with one leg using one crank arm. I realize not everyone wants to endure that level of struggling or that this type of perserverance makes someone more worthy or better than someone else. That being said, if someone enjoys emtb riding, follows the rules for the riding area and has fun who am I to judge. Go for it.
It's because of your age you are almost the age you are actually old and you think you can delay that inevitable situation by telling yourself you don't need it yet. üòÇ  Young people don't care they just want to have fun and buy an ebike cause they can't be arsed getting old any time soon.  It's just the "I'm almost really old and I want to deny it as long as possible" people that don't want them.

I've got nothing against eBikes as a type of bike, I do have issues with the types of riders they attract. For starters eBike kid gangs are funny but at the same time very scary for normal motorists, they fly through stop signs and sidewalks and can really end up as a hood ordainment which would suck for my insurance prems. Next on the list, eBikes reduce the skill barrier for more advanced trails. I've seen more yuppies attempt to hit jump-lines or black diamond trails just because they think speed is what makes people good enough for these trails, only for them to find out that it's not speed but bike skill and when you're bombing DH on an eBike that's 40-50 LBS you carry more momentum than a 20 LBS DH or Enduro bike. So making that off camber blind turn is where I see these weekend warriors wrapped around trees and end up in the ER. eBikes have a place in outdoor sports just like MX but it should be just that, if the local trail system doesn't allow motorcycles then no eBikes.

I'm 52 years old. Until this year, I was fairly fit on the bike (100+ mile MTB rides and 4000+ miles a year). Last year I had a crash that required surgeries, and I had to spend a year not riding. I got very out of shape and put on 45 pounds. I had never considered e-bikes for a lot of the reasons you mentioned. I didn't want it to ruin me for my other bikes, and a large part of why I ride is to stay fit. That being said, now that I am back to riding, I realized I could actually have fun while getting back in shape on an e-bike. I live in Southern California where the climbs are brutal. I picked up a new YT decoy on sale for $3500, and have been having a blast while steadily getting back in shape. The key to this are the e-bike modes! When I need the workout, I force myself to stay in eco mode. This mode just helps a little (50-60 watts of help). When I feel I have earned some fun, or I need a recovery ride, I'll go for a ride in trail mode. I am steadily dropping weight and getting more fit while having a blast and doing rides I'd never be able to do otherwise. Great tool if you have a little mode discipline. My son finds it impossible to keep it in eco mode when he rides it, but I do it no problem. I also ride my other bikes more than the e-bike now that I am getting more fit. My biggest fear of the e-bike making my other bikes less appealing has not proved to be the case at all. This has been my experience. Hopefully this helps other in similar situation.
As a disabled veteran my e-bike has opened the door for me. I can get out more and not have as much of the knee pain that I had ridding my regular mountain bike. I have lost over 50 lbs since upgrading to an e-bike this year. I seriouly think the e-bike has changed my life for the good.

I think there‚Äôs a very different perspective for ebikes in the US vs the UK or EU.  Here in the UK for example if you ride to work you can get as much as 40% off the cost of your bike, and they‚Äôre rarely banned (the legal ones that is, you turn up on a Surron and you‚Äôll be leaving just as quickly). It‚Äôs normal for everyone to just ride together and whilst you still get the old ‚Äòcheat‚Äô every time you pass someone on the climbs it‚Äôs generally tongue in cheek.  The best thing about them isn‚Äôt that they‚Äôre not a workout. It‚Äôs that you are in CONTROL of the workout. Maintaining your heartrate in a specific zone without blowing out is much better than dying on some massive climb and then stopping at the top. With an ebike you just adjust the assistance to keep the effort constant and achievable.  Not just that but if I, for example, sprain something, I know that I can just turbo the bike home and it‚Äôll get me there. Even broke my wrist once and turbo‚Äôed the bike home.  I‚Äôve been riding my Cube for 2 and a half years now, and it‚Äôs the best thing I ever did. They just make you WANT to go ride.

I‚Äôm ok w/ e-bikes, it‚Äôs just that I don‚Äôt want to go there until I absolutely must. I‚Äôm also waiting for them to get lighter and cheaper. At least lighter.
I mountain biked 3-4 times a week, 10 to 15 miles a ride, that was about 3 years ago. I then got covid and 3 years later I still have what is known as long haul covid and my overall ability to keep fit, to even get fit, and probably never get as fit, is gone. The ebike saved mountain biking for me. I can ride again, I enjoy it, and because of the inability for my lungs to get oxygen to my body as needed, ebiking does make me sweat and feel like I have worked out. It is very hilly here in Alabama and when I tried to ride my old hard tail, 1-2 miles was my max and it would land me in bed for 2 days due to post exercise malaise. On my ebike, I can ride 12 to 14 miles, still tired, but I got to do a lot more, have a lot more fun.   The negative of an ebike, is the look I get from other riders because I am on an ebike. I almost want to get a shirt that says 'I used to be you, but I can't do that anymore'. While we mountain bikers might be friendlier than cyclists, there is still a lot of judging going on.   Do I wish I could ride my old bike? Yes. When I move it around the garage, it feels so light and so free, but at this point in life, and maybe never, can I ride my old hardtail. When you can complete one successfully you know; You are fit, you have skills and you have grit. I also like shuttling my son around with toe rope. Both ebike and analog bike compliment each other. Analog bike feels easier to flick around after training with ebike on technical terrain.

I just prefer to do it on my own power, a human powered vehicle. Mostly it's a habit. I don't have a regular vehicle at all. I live in a small city many would consider rural and things are spread out. I built my own bike for less than the Walmart bike, using parts from other bikes and microshift derailleur, shifter buttons and cassette, Amazon shimano clone hydraulic brakes and the graphene enhanced 2.35 tires. Don't have enough money for these expensive bikes. Sewed my own tent, blanket, top quilt and backpack. Pieced together kits for cooking, cleaning, carrying water, etc. The thing floats and cruises along as if there was a motor in it. I forget my old speeds and often forget that it mostly feels the same but I'm moving along much faster nowadays. I prefer backcountry multiuse trails. Only issue I've had so far was with the tight clearance in the back tire and frame in peanutbutter mud and with slush freezing in very low temperature wind chill. Bicycle was historically what gave people freedom. It does not cost me money for gas or hotel rooms when I travel. I can cook my own food and do all or almost all of my own repairs. Needing to keep my bike charged doesn't make sense in this paradigm. I already feel quite conflicted keeping the phone, razor, headlamp and backup batteries charged. I like electronics though. Built my own cnc machine and have computerized embroidery machines, 3d printers, diecutters and laser engravers that all get regular use, maintenance and modification. The experience of riding to my limits has led to peak experiences and helped me to develop as a person, mentally physically, emotionally and perhaps spiritually. I can't speak for anyone else but we might cross upon the same paths but we are not riding the same ride.

Been riding an eMTB for the past several months.  Levo SL 2023.  My "analog" bike is a 2021 Stumpy.  I love both.  As for the fitness thing, I get MORE of a workout on the eMTB with regards to cardio and calories burned according to my Garmin 955.  However, I don't feel as if I'm redlining my legs as much compared to my Stumpy - so not sure my muscles are getting stronger, but don't really have a reliable way to measure that.  However, this requires you to ride the SAME AMOUNT OF TIME as before as opposed to THE SAME AMOUNT OF DISTANCE.  If you just take your normal trails and outings and start riding them on an eBike, you'll finish faster, burn less calories, and spend less time in fat-burning heart rate zones.  I love having both bikes depending on who I'm riding with and what we are riding.  For quick local rides around 8-12mi, I'm happy to ride the analog.  The next thing I'm going to test, however, is really tweaking the 3 quick-access Power/Support Zones on my Levo SL.  At factory settings, Level 1 (Eco) more than compensates for the 10lb weight difference of my old bike while Level 2 (Trail) is so much power that I can barely feel the difference between it and Level 3 (Turbo) and actually makes riding a bit too easy.  I plan on making Level 1 be something that gives me just enough minimum boost to make it feel as close to riding my analog bike as possible with the 10lb weight increase.  I plan on making Level 2 be somewhere between that and that is currently and then making Level 3 be what my Level 2 is today.

But why do you never use a mud guard
Lot of people tend to think e-bikes are "cheating".  Did we agree to a race? The only person you can cheat if you ride recreationally is yourself. You can pedal an e-bike as hard as you want. It CAN be easier if you want it to be, but it can also be exactly as hard as riding your regular old whip is.   Again, you can pedal it as hard as you want to. I'm a big guy, I float between about 230 and 270 pounds depending on how hard I am riding. Where I live, in Oregon, riding up trail is absolutely brutal for someone my size. So here is a side benefit of an emtb. It's like a band for doing pull ups. If you can't do a pull up, you might use a band to give you a little bit of a self spot. Eventually, you can do 1 pull up unassisted. Then you can do 2, before you know it, you can do 10-15-20 unassisted. An emtb can be used exactly the same way. Jump on the emtb, dial in the power profile so the bike is giving you JUST enough oompf that you don't go anaerobic and blow up on every single punchy climb. Do that for a few months, and then jump on your daily rider, and all of a sudden, you're riding up a trail you had to walk significant parts of before.  Grinding 300 watts on an hour long climb on a hard tail or a regular old full squish on one lap, is the same as grinding 300 watts up that same climb twice because it takes you 30 minutes to do instead. Sure, you can slap it in boost or trail or whatever, and then soft pedal, but that says more about you than it does about the bike. Like anything, it's a tool. If you want to use it for fitness, you can. If you want to use it because you don't like the pain cave? That's cool too. Me? I'll be blasting up hill flat out, dying inside, and loving the pain cave, but also loving the fact I can do two laps instead of one, before I need to go have a lie down :)  Lighter people might not understand this, but, I tend to get a better workout on my heckler than I do on my honzo. The reason is pretty simple if you think about it. There isn't much leeway in terms of taking your foot off the gas when you're 270 pounds and climbing 2,000 feet in 4 miles. It's pretty much all gas, no brakes the whole way up, and by the time you get to the top, you're absolutely blasted. You want, no, you NEED a nap :) With the ebike, I'm still smashing uphill, but I have the wiggle room to take the foot off the gas a bit so I don't blow up. That means I can get in more cardio at say, 2 hours at 80% threshold, instead of 1 hour at 90+% threshold.
I understand "punishment" thing, but still want emtb. Why? I usually go on a 3 hour up-down session (average trail lenght 800m), but as hours passing I feel less and less capable during descent. I just use so much energy for an ascent. That's where emtb shines. It allowes ride much longer on descents, by preserving the energy.
I prefer using name "analog mountain bike" than "normal mountain bike" :)

I own all acoustic bikes at 50 years old and I will wait until I MUST own an eMTB.  That said they are AWESOME to rent when you are traveling for work and you want to cover as much ground as possible in a couple of days.  I just returned from Boise, ID and I had a blast on the Specialized Levo Carbon (a big bike), that was quite heavy, but had a darn good range. I climbed 3400 feet and returned back to my hotel 29 miles later on an empty battery. It limits the trails you can ride dramatically in Boise, though. I recommend that anyone who turns their nose up at eBikes go rent one sometime.  I still have no interest in owning one anytime soon, but I do appreciate them now as a great way to explore new territory on vacation when I want to cover more ground and don't know the routes well enough to plan a just-right day.  In that circumstance a little bit of cheating is just right.  And having a motor is always fun, even if it's now always what you want.
One additional benefit of riding an eBike is that it will preserve your body to an extent and extend the time you are able to ride a regular mountain bike. There really is a limited amount of wear and tear that your body can take. Some of it comes from the roughness of a trail, but some comes from really exerting yourself to your limits. I think a mix would be good. I'm nearing 60 and still have those invincibility moments, but they're fairly rare these days! What I feel like I've learned *and* come to accept is that we have an incredibly strong attachment to the physical aspects of youth, and it can actually work against you in the long run. I would say it's better to add an eBike to the mix before you feel like you *need* an eBike. This is probably even more true for a youtuber with an inside line on bike equipment.

Most of the time when you climb on an ebike you get to the top so quickly you barely have time to get out of breath. Trick to maintaining cardio is to find longer and steeper climbs, do longer loops with more hills or just do multiple laps of your regular loops and put in my max effort at all times. Do this and do maybe one 'acoustic' bike ride ever couple of weeks with climbs and sprints and you'll barely lose any of your cardio but you'll get 3, 4, 5 (or more) times as much descending in be as you used to. Plus when you ride your DH bike at the park it feels like a racing bmx with the weight difference to the eeb. Best part is the sheer amount of ground I can cover and all the mountains I've been able to climb since I got one without being out all day long from dawn til dusk and being dead the next day.

I would love to get an E-Bike, they're just too expensive still.  It would be hard for me to justify say, 1500-2000 dollars for a decent mountain bike that's a step up from entry level (entry level I think is a rip-off and you're better off going used or upgrading a wal-mart bike), but when you add the electric motor 1500-2000 dollars seems a LOT more reasonable, because you're getting a lot more for your money.  You're not getting a fancier version of a thing that costs 300 bucks at walmart and paying an extra 1-2 thousand for it... you're getting a VEHICLE, a machine, something that - personally, at 36 and not in great shape anymore, with kids, without a lot of time - would get me out on my bike a lot more often, because instead of having to spend so much time training and getting in shape just to do basic rides, I could instantly start having fun any time I wanted to ride it.  That's a huge plus for normies.  I already know how to ride fairly well from when I was younger, but now my fitness just isn't there.  But give me an E-Bike, and I'm back at it again like I was 20. I'm not very fit, couldn't last more than 30mins on a MTB, I converted it to an eMTB and can now go for 3hour rides which gets me out of the house, exploring more, and I maintain an avg heart rate of around 150bpm.  The assistance means I don't have to worry about having a heart attack when going up hill and can take little moving breaks and enjoy the colling effect of some extra wind.  I don't do many trails, just bike paths.
for me, the appeal is my asthma. Inhaler or not, no matter my cardio level as a medic, I just feel miserable when I'm climbing a hill on a bike. I don't get that endorphin rush from exercise like others - for me, it's a difficult task, and working out is a chore I have to suffer through every week. So the draw to mountain biking to me is getting out into the mountains, not the difficulty of the workout. I don't even really love riding a ton on my normal bikes in hilly areas. But once I got my first city ebike, the whole activity changed for me. I now ride EVERYWHERE. I rode to and from school, I ride to the grocery store and get groceries, I ride to go get prescriptions, I'll ride with my dog (who really wants to be going 20mph all the time) It opened up areas of Seattle that were frankly impossible due to the hills, and I can get places without being sweaty (which is a perk in city riding). I'm putting in hundreds of miles a month now. my wife is the same, she went from zero to a daily rider. And we don't just max assist/throttle everywhere. The assist never really comes off the lowest level unless it's a steep hill. It's just those bursts of heavy work being lowered down that keeps the activity encouraging.  I'd genuinely be interested in an e-mtb if they weren't still so extremely frowned upon in so many places by gatekeepers who don't understand that people differ in physical ability. but I will say that having a $1000 ride1up core5 and roadster v2 (walmart even makes a half decent ebike by hyper for $400) has transformed how much bike related exercise I get, not needing to worry about making sure I use my inhaler 25 minutes before every time I go out and being able to effortlessly go further has been nothing short of transformative.  You should try getting one of walmart's hyper e-mountain bikes with a 250w hub motor, and seeing if you can transform it into a usable product like with the first kent.

I live in a City and I love Mountainbiking, so I love my E-MTB. Since I have it I seldom need my car. I ride to work with it and arrive in the office without sweating and being exhausted and I get out of to the city to the nice trails in the woods or to the next trail park, without having to swallow too much car exhaust. When I go for a ride with it I'm usually riding at least 50 km and 3-4 hours. As soon as I'm out of the city, I'm at least warmed up and I decrease the motor support and I climb uphill trails which I would never do on a normal bike. In average I burn about 1500 calories on each tour.

I know that an ebike is most probably in my future, if I want to continue this hobby. But they need to become way lighter, for me to even consider them. 4:45 This is how urban ebikes differ wildly from emtbs. With emtbs you pedal just as hard to go farther and ride longer, but ultimately you're speed limited due to the terrain whereas a urban ebike can be pushed to 60+km/h and actually give you a good workout and reduce your commuting time. Of course I'm not talking about the 5kW motorcycles, but the 250-1000W ebikes out there that give you just enough extra speed without removing you from the equation.

I used to ride Cube AMS all carbon xc bike for probably 12 years. I loved that bike, but as I got older, I started developing issues with knees, where I was unable to continue riding after climbing a starter hill (10km continuous climb). After 18 years of bike riding, I was done... well, until I bought an ebike. I didnt want to get an ebike at first, I planned to buy new trail bike, but I discovered ebike at the same price and so I bought it. And ever since I got it, I rode even more and more often than before. Longer trails, stepper climbs, harder rides, etc. It made a lot difference for me, and I even ride it in much colder temperatures, than I would ride my old bike before.   I'll be 35 soon and I hear a lot from other people that ebikes are for old farts, but screw them. Ebike brought me back to cycling and enabled me to go much further than before. I now go in the hills every other day after work, doing 40-50km per ride, I'm faster at climbs and have much more time to enjoy donwhill trails. I havent been to bike park with it yet, I would probably rent a regular bike for that anyways, but for trails and hills I have close by , Im really happy to have an ebike.  I still have my old carbon xc, I renovated it and use it as a commute bike to work these days. I tried to go the same road as I do with ebike and while I made it all the way, the climb was way too slow and much less enjoyable after 2 years of pure ebike riding..  So it really depends. If you are a rider that goes to trailparks a lot, ebike is probably not for you. But If you have hills with tons of trails, just barely 1km away from your flat and you want to get some action after work, ebike might be a valid option for you :)

Our rules here in Australia are a bit different to the US I believe - our e-bikes are all pedal assist, peak power limited, and the motor cuts out at I think 25 kph - basically bicycles that help people ride up hills. They fit in pretty well with other riders generally. It seems (looking in from over here), there are a lot of e-bikes in the US that are basically electric motos with [optional] pedals, that would be kinda sketchy - and they are treated like motos in Australia.  When I get too old to ride analog, I will absolutely have an e-bike or two to keep enjoying riding.

I resisted the temptation for a long time but bought one shortly after turning 60 this year.  For me, with a decent but not great fitness level and an increasingly-uncooperative right knee, the ebike has been a game-changer.  On a regular bike, chasing my more fit friends through the woods is certainly great exercise but also more a study in grim determination than fun.  The ebike shifts the balance more towards fun and less towards exercise.  I wish I had bought one years ago.

I live on a glorified sandbar, with almost zero hills. Those hills that we have are just gentle slopes. Even though I am an old Boomer, (but not a MAMIL), I see no need to get an e-assist on my bikes.

The problem with this video is that you're only reviewing full fat e-bikes. Modern lightweight e-bikes pretty much negate every critique you had here. The weight when heading downhill and jumping and such isn't nearly as noticeable on a 40lb lightweight e-bike, and you definitely will get a work out only getting 30-60Nm of assist. I was outright anti e-bike until I spent a weekend with a buddy's Rise... I was riding further and faster, and riding with a higher average heart rate and more energy expenditure over the ride. The big difference was that I wasn't blowing myself up on climbs to waste five minutes recovering before hitting the descents, I was motoring up the descent with a respectable assist, getting to the top after some exertion but just rolling straight into the descents and having a way more enjoyable ride overall. They're not for everybody, and if I wasn't putting a few thousand miles in on the road every year I might have more guilt over it but, for me, a lightweight e-bike is the most fun way to experience the trails.

Planning on getting an eMTB for next season. Main reason for doing so is to extend our rides. I'm not the fittest guy around, and have no issues riding 2-3 hours on my current MTB in the local trails, but I feel it physically. Occasionally my friends and I want to travel to explore trails outside the immediate area we live, that that required 1-2 hours driving one-way. So when you start doing the math of drive time vs ride time, having an eMTB at my disposal makes those day trips more worth it.   One example, we travel to a mountain that's 90 min door to trail. On my current MTB I'll take 15-20 min riding up to the top, another 10-15 recuperating, and 2-3 minutes down. After 5-6 runs, I'm physically done with the climbing bit, and that is a full day adventure. Recently we rented eMTB's to visit the same mountain, using mid-level assist (not turbo mode), I managed 10 runs, physically I could have gotten more in, but we got a later start to the day than we normally do, and were running out of daylight,  we also had to return the bikes prior to closing time (rental closed before the mountain).  Physically I felt like I could do another 5 runs if the time permitted.   The other benefit we look forward to us longer distance in our local 2-3 hour rides. It will allow us to go further, explore more trails that are out of our current reach. It lets us experience more of the local trails than what our current physical limitations allow us.  Looking forward to have both a MTB and eMTB at my disposal, where my MTB will be more useful in the slower more technical trails when more power/weight is more hinderance.

For me the reason not to get an E-MTB is that none have the two features that could convince me: regenerative braking and front-wheel drive. As Seth says, the "makes it easier" aspect of E-bikes actually is a negative. I know myself: I _need_ properly tough climbs - I can also never quite convince myself to give my everything when I'm in a place with no hills >100 m. The aspect that would be positive is the "do things I couldn't otherwise do". But the available E-bikes wouldn't really help with the frontiers I actually encounter, or be outright counterproductive: carrying up stairs etc., going on 300 km backcountry trips, getting stuck in bogs or snow. A really light E-drive on the front that helps only in the toughest spots and completely recharges itself on the next descent, that would be the thing.  Oh, and the other reason not to go electric is that too many E-mountainbikers are jerks who rip up the trails in the wet, scare hikers and look down on riders who "only" ride an acoustic bike!

Watching this has been fascinating for me as somebody who lives in the middle of the bike kingdom in Switzerland. E-MTBs are the norm in my neighbourhood and I ride a Canyon Strive:ON in our woods daily. Trailheads here still mainly ride what we call "Bio-Bikes" (translated to biological bikes - MTBs without assist), but mainly because they've been riding for a long time already, since it's the most popular hobby here besides hiking. Our environment is very hilly, heck, we're in the middle of a valley almost anywhere you live in my "state", so a task as simple as riding home from work in your lunch break on a bike without assist is one hell of an Olympic workout if you don't live in the bottom of the valley.  Delivery vehicles aren't called "E-Bikes" here, more the likes of "E-Scooters", and when you say you ride an E-Bike, people here would think that you're either riding an E-MTB or an assisted commuter bike.
e-bike is definitely more of a slow burn workout. I'll ride all-day with a 110-130 heart rate, vs a 1.5 hour ride peaking 170-180bpm at times. Also doing 6-7 laps instead of 2-3.
What would be the point of an e-mtb? The electric portion is for extending range and speed of the bike to get places that would be difficult with human power alone. MTB is a sport, a physical sport. Adding an electric motor makes it a motor sport. Now it's just motorcross.

I live in an urban area, and mountain biking used to be my escape.  I could be away from people, and alone with my thoughts, mostly thanks to my fitness and being able to ride further from civilization than most.  Ebikes have taken that from me.  I can't stand going to my local trails on a Saturday morning because it is filled with way more people, bringing a lot of inexperienced (inconsiderate) riders. The trails are now dust in the early summer when they used to be that way at the end of the season. If you're limited by age or medical conditions, that's a valid excuse for an ebike.  Otherwise, you're robbing yourself (and me) of the experience of real biking.

If you do dive into eMTBs, give the "lite" eMTBs a try. Lower power motors are extremely natural feeling. Still make you work for the climb, heart rate will still be way up, but just take the edge off so your joints aren't quite as sore the next day. Specialized Levo SL, Scott Lumen, Orbea Rise, Trek EXe just to name a few. Most also come with smaller/lighter batteries making "lite" eMTB Trail bikes overall weight similar to an alloy Enduro MTB.  Just because you're on an eBike doesn't mean you need to always run it at max assistance.

Great video Seth! While NWA might be utopia for e-bikers in the sense that everything is open to Class 1 bikes it's also a bit subjective: The climbs aren't really that long and you're basically at sea level (plenty of oxygen). Also the trails (even the techy uphills) are SO well constructed they're fairly rideable by a skilled mountain biker. In some areas out west e-bikes can shine on terrain that is uniquely distinct. Think pedalling up at 12,000+' through loose scree that only someone like Nino Schurter might clean. Or here in Moab climbing up near vertical slickrock that pushes the envelope of what's rideable. If you live in an area that's relatively flat or more rolling hills e-bikes make less sense and are harder to prove their worth. In high altitude zones or areas with extremely challenging terrain they can really shine in that department.

Mountain bikers of all ages that have an advanced riding level like you, Seth, find it difficult to justify riding an ebike. If they are older (I'm 66) like me, with ailments such as a hip replacement, degenerating discs in my neck and displaced discs in my lumber spine easily justify riding an ebike. With the pain during and after a ride on my enduro I'm limited to 4-6 miles. Climbs become daunting and I don't enjoy the ride as much, let alone the pain for the next week I must endure and try to manage. My son is 46 and is in great shape so he often had to wait for me at the top of a climb. But with my ebike I can stay up with him and even have a conversation. I still get a decent workout by keeping the assist mode is "Eco", the lowest mode of assist. Later in the ride I may need to increase the assist one level to conquer a lengthy steep climb. I rarely use the "Turbo" mode, if ever. My average speed on rides is usually 9 or 10 miles an hour so I'm not blazing up the hill recklessly, like some I've seen. And going downhill with my skill level I don't go any faster than when I'm on my enduro, well, because I'm old and don't heal as quickly after a crash. It sounds like I'm lobbying for more areas to allow ebikes so riders like me can enjoy new trails. I always enjoy your videos, Seth. Looking forward to the next one. Bob

5:43 "...but my cardio is just gonna go to heck."  I'd say an ebike is actually better for cardio as it allows you to train at a lower heart rate, which is the goal for zone 1 and 2 training. And for anyone trying to improve their cardio, lower heart rate training should be the main focus at about 60-80% of all training.   For pedaling power and maximum power output training they might not be as good as they allow you to slack off, but as you said it's up to the rider.

Ebike owner here. Would not think about going back to acoustic biking. I won't tell you what to buy but I will talk about my experience with 2 ebikes I've owned. I currently have a Specialized Levo SL. I bought it for the weight reduction (about 40lbs) and for the mission control mobile app. It lets me change the power assist in 5% increments and save multitudes of presets. This is a must in my opinion as you can fine tune your assist to meet the pace of the group (or your goal). My old ebike was a Cannondale moterra (60lbs). It was a more powerful motor but the group I ride with I did not need all that power. It only had 4 assist levels, no customization so I was either too fast or too slow for the group.   My skill and confidence has skyrocketed since owning an ebike. I saw more improvement in a year than I did over several years before. I can ride faster and be less tired. I was making at speed attempts at jumps. Doing doubles I would never have been able to clear. Most importantly I was less tired so I find myself jumping over roots and rocks. With the added weight to the bike jumps felt more safe to me. My first bike was a marin hawkhill and on any jump or other feature I would tend to over correct.  If you ride once or twice a week I highly recommend an ebike. The cheapest I've found is $2700 giant talon e+, a hardtail. Or $3500 stance e+, a full suspension. Optionally if you already own am acoustic bike, you can purchase an ebike kit, they are about $1000. Make sure it is a mid drive ebike kit.

The trick to owning a Ebike is not giving in to riding it exclusively. I had a similar fear that getting one would ruin me for riding my other bikes.  Last year, I was recovering from covid and found that was a benefit having a little assist, but also found  Emtbs to be a crap ton of fun. Then, I started finding the Emtb was really building my cornering skills because of the extra weight and the fact that I could allocate some of my energy from pedaling to bike handling. Switching back to my regular bike, I found myself maintaining higher cornering speeds because I was now allocating energy I was expending into bike handling back into pedaling higher speeds. I recently tried an e-bike at a demo day at the local trails here in north Georgia.  I'm 63 years old, in decent enough shape, but not winning any races.  I tried out a Santa Cruz ebike.  My thoughts on it were first that it was a massively heavy bike- going from a 34 pound bike to a 52 pound bike is... strange.  I didn't turn the thing up all the way, I kept in in the 1 and 2 settings.  Normally I average about 6mph on the trail, with a max speed of 18 or so.  On the demo ride, I averaged 10.2mph, with a top speed of 28mph.  To say it was easier is an understatement.  I was trying to keep up some effort, and found the only way to do that was to go faster, or turn the thing off.  I hear a lot of people saying "you get the same workout on an ebike", and that's rubbish, unless you're going twice as far or twice as fast.  Maybe someday I'll get an e-mtb, but not today.  My conclusion was that it made me a lot faster, and therefore a lot more dangerous.  My last broken arm was in my late 50s, and at my age healing isn't that easy.  :)  I'll stick to my 'acoustic' for now, get an e-bike if and when I ever can't make it up the hill.

However, in USA where E-bikes are not accepted or allowed in many places, I can completely understand not owning one. The main problem is the laws are not as strict in the US as they are in Europe, and therefore the bikes can go faster or even have throttle assist, which makes them more dangerous on trails shared by walkers. The strict laws where assist is cut at 25km / h, and it is occasionally controlled by police, has the advantage that e-Bikes can go more places and are more accepted here in Europe. I've been a hardcore MTB-er for 40 yrs now. I got a Trek Rail 7 emtb two years ago and have never looked back. I ride all the same trails I rode in my regular mtb days, but I just go faster and further in the same ride time. The big difference is that I'm ALWAYS, ALWAYS up for a ride... any ride. EMTB's are outdoor trainers. User choice means you can spin at 90+ and get a great work out or you can take it easier. For newer riders skills are easier to learn, rides will be longer and users build endurance. EMTB's make you faster on your regular bike and your regular bike makes you faster on your EMTB.  I would point out that EMTB's are allowed in all NC state forests and Federal Parks and only in designated USFS lands. In North Carolina ebikes (class 1: low speed electric vehicles) are specifically defined in the motor vehicle statutes as not being motor vehicles. It's the same for federal consumer law. Congress defined the interstate commerce definition as "not a motor vehicle". USFA and other public entities have policies that define the product as a motor vehicle but it's not based in law which is why it will change as the social acclimation takes place.

Hi Great video and good review of eMTBs. I'm 63 in the UK ride a Trek Remedy 9.8 and Specialized SL gen 1. I previously had a Turbo Levo but was never comfortable on it going downhill, too heavy. I tried the SL because it was cheap (used). Didn't think it would cut the mustard but immediately became my go-to bike. I got the Levo when my health and fitness were on a downward slide. I've dropped 11kg in the last year so the Remedy now feels like an eMTB but the SL is still my go-to. I usually ride it in eco with a bit trail if I'm on my own but it's great having the safety net of trail/turbo if I'm having a bad day or run out of puff, infrequent but happens as you get on a bit. I do agree 90nm 500w peak power ebikes are a different activity. IMO they don't integrate so well with riders on "acoustic" bikes. Doable but not so good.
Once you don't just do mountainbiking on an eMTB, they make a hell of a lot of sense. Most cities just aren't bike-ready or even bike-friendly yet. Having to ride through potholes and within heavy car traffic in the rain and snow - it really is nice to be able to just pull away at the lights and keep a steady 27 km/h even on uphill sections AND have enough suspension to not get scattered.  I have seen an "Urban eBike" (no suspension) being pushed by a car while avoiding a small pothole. It was 100% the car driver's fault (no safety distance whatsoever), of course, but it'd still be the biker under the wheel. Suspension is everything. And yes, cars have to be generally denied access to cities, but right now, things are the way they are.

In Europe they have 2 classes of e-bikes. 25kmh or 15.5mph are considered bicycles and can go anywhere a bicycle can. 45kmh or 27.9mph is considered a light motorcycle and needs a license plate and are not allowed on bike trails or lanes unless the motor is off. Some might see that as limiting but I feel it‚Äôs a good compromise to keep people flying up trails like a dirt bike or motorcycle and keep the bicycle feeling.

Seth, thank you for your open approach towards ebikes. In my personal opinion, I think ebikes are a very nice way to include people, just as you explained. And they have other applications. They support a parent hauling their kid in a trailer when it's just a means of transportation. They fuel a cargo bike when people move around goods in an urban area. They help weaker people get their breath of fresh air and outdoor activity. Heck, they even help office workers get to their job without sweating. But - for me - I still need a *reason* to use electric assistance. Because electric assistance has downsides which I don't want when not absolutely necessary. It's heavy. Heavy to load onto your car. Heavy to carry into your basement. Heavy to put on your maintenance rack. Heavy to pull around when riding. It's expensive. You always have to pay for every single part of your bicycle. When an ebike costs the same as a conventional bike, I assume the conventional bike has higher quality components. And mostly: An ebike will strip me from the feeling of achievement. When I'm cycling, breathing hard, sweating, legs are burning, everything is getting dirty, that's what I did. When I get home from my daily commute, I know what I achieved. When I haul my little one to the kindergarten, I love to wave goodbye to ebikers while I pass them with a whistle on my lips, because *I can.* I am proud of my strong legs and what they can do for me, and I'm not paying thousands to give that away. But - as stated above - I see and respect the reasons why people choose ebikes.

Great video and you covered almost every aspect that explains why e-MTBs bikes are so popular.  As a 64-year-old E-MTB rider (on my 4th year on one) I feel that you "got it" (as you say) except perhaps on the subject of cardio. You see, in the days of me riding an acoustic/"proper" (as you called it) mountain bike, I would often (on climbs) reach a point where my heart-rate would go through the roof at which point my younger/fitter buddies would either have to wait for me to "recover" or wave goodbye as they watch return to base. Now, with the e-bike, at the beginning I was cranking up the power and feeling like Superman until one day I said "Wait a minute I'm no longer doing much cardio" and I have now adopted a methodology which gives me both cardio/sweat as well as the great feeling that you experienced going uphill.  I start off in "Eco" mode (the minimum assistance on my Bosch-powered Trek e-bike where most of the assistance is used to carry the extra weight of the bike) and I watch my heart rate. When I get to the point where I have reached the heart-rate that I have decided would be the maximum, I then crank up the assistance to the next level (Tour) or even the one after that ("EMTB" mode on Bosch) and then slowly watch my heart rate drop to the level that I am comfortable at which point I would switch back to Eco or Tour whichever mode is the one below the current. That way I do get a workout, I don't give up and go home, I ride with my buddies, I ride longer and higher and also get a better range from my battery. Overall, in my case I am getting a better workout in a typical e-ride than I was on my acoustic bike.  Keep up the great videos - a subscriber from Cyprus.

That so stupid not to let people ride ebikes. Or in general to have special areas where you are allowed to ride mountain bikes and not let people just ride where ever is forest and trails.   But about ebikes, if you ride easy or built tracks it tends to be lot easier with ebike but on natural trails that are at least here in Finland usually really technical riding ebike is just as hard in terms of hear rate etc. You don't need to pedal so much but stuff just comes towards you so fast that it makes normal riding bit similar to endure/downhill, so more pumping etc. full body movement instead on putting most of effort on pedaling.    So it's definitely different sport, sometimes easier some times just as hard or even harder than normal mountain biking.   In my mind the main negative side is how much the bikes wear down from more riding and faster speeds on hard trails. So the bikes are not super expensive but owning one surely is :D

most of the evidence shows that you will exercise more often and for longer if you own an e-bike. I definitely get more exercise since I got an e-bike because I use it to go for leisurely rides on my rest days.
I feel like the biggest strength of e-bikes in general is lowering the barrier to entry for cycling. If someone wants to commute on a bike but can't make 15 miles a day (yet?) or wants to start mountain biking but just can't make the climbs and still enjoy the ride, then an e-bike can allow them to start out without having to spend months or years getting to the point that they can make the minimum to have fun.   The big problem is people getting going faster than they're really capable of handling, and the fact that a lot of so-called ebikes are basically just unregulated mopeds, and are ridden by people who wouldn't ever ride a regular bicycle.   Also you should get a pet lizard. Trust me, they're great.
